<div id="review_172749670" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172749670" />
    <a title="Cogito_ergo_sum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5546503-cogito-ergo-sum"><img alt="Cogito_ergo_sum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370246091p2/5546503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172749670?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cogito_ergo_sum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cogito_ergo_sum" href="/user/show/5546503-cogito-ergo-sum">Cogito_ergo_sum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5546503-cogito-ergo-sum?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5546503-cogito-ergo-sum?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172749670" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6250600740810504109">Update Jan 2014.<br/>Upon rereading this book, years later, just because I was reminiscing, I found that the story just doesn't do it for me any more. I am pulling the rating down from 4 stars to 2 stars. I first read this book Aug. 2010.<br/><br/>The writing, while it did put the main point across, wasn't satisfactory. The author failed to describe the town/city Lori lived in. The fact that I don't know if it's a city or town or suburbs says something. All I know is that it had a lake or something, and rich p</span>
  <span id="freeText6250600740810504109" style="display:none">Update Jan 2014.<br />Upon rereading this book, years later, just because I was reminiscing, I found that the story just doesn't do it for me any more. I am pulling the rating down from 4 stars to 2 stars. I first read this book Aug. 2010.<br /><br />The writing, while it did put the main point across, wasn't satisfactory. The author failed to describe the town/city Lori lived in. The fact that I don't know if it's a city or town or suburbs says something. All I know is that it had a lake or something, and rich people lived there off to one side. This ambiguity is irritating, since I have no idea where the main character grew up. That would give me more insight into her character. All I know is that her family is well off, probably upper middle class with her dad being a lawyer, and their owning waterfront property. The only thing the author described is the hotness surrounding the boys, and not much else. I've never seen wake-boarding, so I had no idea what the tricks the author was describing were supposed to look like. If I have to put a book down to go look up a video of what a trick looks like, since I have no idea what I'm supposed to be picturing, it's not very good description. <br /><br />I found the main character kind of annoying, which is a problem since this book was written in first person. However, the dialogue between the characters seemed very genuine to me, therefore believable. So there's a point towards that. And the author did a great job of showing the relationship between siblings. Though I have no idea how real it is--I've never seen a relationship between brothers. I hope it's not this bad in real life. I believe the teasing, but I hope the malice is purely fictional. That would be very sad, and stressful. <br /><br />As for character development, there wasn't much. I didn't leave the story with a feeling of taking something away. That feeling where you connect with the main character, suffer with the main character, hope for the best, see the main character change, and at the end, it changed you. No. Don't expect anything like that. <br /><br />The plot was amusing, which is probably the reason I liked the book so much back then. The plot was ridiculous and was intended for comedic purposes--the same kind of comedy you'd expect from a sitcom. Would never happen in real life, but, on a stage, very funny. I thought it was a very creative plot too.<br /><br />I'm going to finish my review with recommending this book to teens (the intended audience anyways). It is very light-hearted. I wouldn't recommend it to adults though. The writing, in my opinion, is not up to par, and the subject matter is too juvenile.<br />------------------------------------------------<br /><br />update: here is a much deeper review of part 1. I had forgotten I had already reread this in 2014, and I went ahead and re-read part 1 only. This extended review only elaborates, with examples, my issues with this book.<br /><br />2 stars.<br /><br />(Reviewed in 2016) <br /><br />Sigh. How time and experience completely changes one’s viewpoint and shatters what was once a rose-coloured memory. Let me <i>elaborate</i>.<br /><br />I’ve read this book waaaaay back in 2007/2008. At the time, I’d rated it 4 stars. I’d found it hilarious and romantic. I wanted to revisit those feelings, that experience, and so I picked this book up again.<br /><br />And my what a <i>shocker</i> it was to find that this book was not the way I remembered it.<br /><br />I’m going to start off with the first thing I noticed (not the most important, but important none the less):<br /><br />The writing. Oh God, the writing.<br /><br />It was simplistic and it lacked a lot of things. Like good description for one. Yes, there is some description, enough to give you a very vague idea, but after reading so many books across many different genres from many different authors, I had developed a good sense of what good writing looks like, and this is not it. <br /><br />The author was poor at setting the scene. Really. I had a hard time picturing things. I didn’t know what anything looked like and I couldn’t picture the characters. I had to make up things in my mind to somewhat ground the story in pictures for myself. I’ve heard of writers talking about leaving some things up to the reader’s imagination, but this was overkill. I should not have to struggle to picture a story. <br /><br />Furthermore, I don’t live around wakeboards and waterskis and sailboats. I have never seen wakeboard tricks. The author didn’t make that much effort in describing the tricks, mostly using wakeboard trick names that meant nothing to me. And when she did describe the action, it was lackluster.<br /><br /><blockquote>I tried a few standbys—a front flip, a scarecrow. There was no busting of ass. So I tried a backroll. And landed it solidly.<br /><br />Now I got cocky. I did a heelside backroll with a nose-grab. This meant that in the middle of the flip, I let go of the rope handle with one hand, reached down, and grabbed the front of the board.</blockquote> <br /><br />Here’s another example. I’ve never been or seen trucking, nor do I <i>want</i> to go. In the story, the characters visit and spend time in some sort of trucking area. Aside from a description of mud (way later in the scene—would have been good to know early on, so that I could have corrected the picture in my head)—I wasn’t told anything else. I still have no idea what this trucking area looks like. Is it unreasonable for me to ask that I read things where the author doesn’t expect the reader to have 1) first hand knowledge of it or 2) have to go to the internet to look up pictures and video references of it? Because I think not. <br /><br />Maybe these events are common where the author lived, but you can’t assume this knowledge will be shared by all readers across North America (or English speakers across the world--no, I'm not excluding you guys in other continents, but I'm limiting my example to North America to keep things simple). Even though we all mostly speak English, carry cell phones and music players, know what modern houses look like, and are familiar with the western culture, the diversity of the geography across North America is so vast that by extension it would affect the way the area would look and what activities the persons living in the area would be familiar with and what the sub-culture in the area is like. (E.g. the historical background of a town/city could drastically change the building architectures in an area. For example, the older section of Montreal looks very different than the rest of it, like older Europe.) So, yes, more details are absolutely necessary. (By the way, I have no idea what the characters’ homes looked like either. What type of houses they lived in. Just another thing to add to the imagery I was struggling with). <br /><br />I want more. Really. <br /><br />If good fantasy writers can make me picture magic and impossible things, and good historical writers can make me believe I’m sitting in the 18th century, I see no reason why I shouldn’t expect the same level of descriptive realism from a contemporary novel about a sport that I have no experience with. <br /><br />Not only that but the lack of descriptive writing extended beyond scene and setting into action. In the second scene, Lori (the main character) is having breakfast with her big brother and father. I can’t picture this. Nothing. Not their posture. Not their expressions. Not the table. Anything. The writer’s main focus appeared to be solely on dialogue and the main character making narrative remarks about her opinion about what is happening (being said) and what she thinks of the people.<br /><br />The action/scene description goes from that bad (what I’ve described in the last paragraph) to somewhat tolerable, but nowhere near “good.” In some places, the poor action description even led to confusion.<br /><br />For example, in one scene (late in the book), we are told there is a big splash. I had assumed something else was happening, like <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ both characters trying to dive and retrieve the necklace in the lake <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, but the main character’s words said otherwise.<br /><br /><blockquote>And then, out of the corner of my eye, I saw a Blur, and Adam was gone. An enormous splash backed everyone away from the water. Adam and Sean flailed in the lake.<br /><br />“Get their parents,” I said […] (and a long paragraph that eventually confirms that they are fighting).</blockquote><br /><br />Flailing, to me, doesn’t mean fighting. If the author had wrote the words “I saw a fist flying” instead (or something of the sort), then I wouldn’t have been dragged out of the story when I realized that there was actually a fight going on. See? Lack of proper description leads to confusion.<br /><br />On a side note, I was put off by the few comic book type exclamations in the narrative. <br /><i>Another coat of mascara and a run-through with the comb attachment to McGillicuddy’s razor and I was ready, baby. <b>Snap!</b></i><br /><br />Despite the problems with the description, there was some good in the writing: good dialogue. It was believable and sounded like real people conversing. This I really liked.<br /><br />However, had this good dialogue been attached to very developed characters, it would have been even better. While these characters were believable, I have met such people, they weren’t deep enough. There was plenty of space for the author to explore other aspects of the characters’ lives. The only thing the author bothered to explore was the rivalry between Sean and Adam. This was really good and believable, but because everything else was… well let’s just leave it at undeveloped, the story was only so-so. Like the saying goes, you are only as strong as your weakest link. <br /><br />Here are many things I would have liked to have been explored: Lori’s depression from the sixth grade, Lori’s feelings about her dead mother, Lori’s relationship with her dad, Adam’s ADHD, and why the heck Lori’s never made a girlfriend in all her years in school. According to her, she was friends with Adam (her age) but they never hung out. She tagged along with the boys in the summer. I know she’s a tomboy but I don’t see how that would have stopped her from forming a close friendship with a girl at school. I don’t understand why her first friendship is at age sixteen with a girl who was only <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ using her to get closer to her big brother. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Lori (the narrator) was beyond irritating. The drama is entirely of her own making and she didn’t appear to have learned her lesson in the end. It, the subterfuge and trickery, was her go to method to try and fix a situation caused by it in the first place. (For clarification, it’s when she agrees to go to the party with <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Sean and make out with him to make Adam jealous and take her back. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>)<br /><br />Hence, poor character development as well.<br /><br />Furthermore, not only is the reason Lori believes she was meant to be with Sean ridiculous and misconstrued (cue my inability to suspend disbelief), but I was never convinced for Lori’s reason of continuing to like Sean. She had said that he <i>lights up the room.</i> Not once had the author shown me this. All I’ve witnessed was Sean being a jerk and bullying his little brother. Lori didn’t like Sean’s mean jokes at Adam (the little brother) but she pretended to laugh anyway because she <i>liked</i> Sean and he <i>”lit up the room.”</i> There wasn’t a single instance to convince me that from Lori’s point of view Sean was super. All I heard was heresy: Sean is nice to me. Sean is surrounded by everybody. Everybody loves Sean. I just want to see one scene where Sean actually is as great (compelling, regardless of being an asshole) as Lori says he is. Apparently, even the brothers’ parents believe Sean over Adam (when Sean was in the wrong). I want to see these scenes with my own eyes. I want to see this natural ability that he has to compel people over to his side. Otherwise, all that’s happening is I’m sitting there, wondering about why Lori’s not getting her head checked when there is no evidence to support Sean’s greatness. This kind of thing is what makes stories lose credibility and turn readers off.<br /><br />Lori’s antics, while they were funny when I was much younger, aren’t so funny now. The entire situation made me want to quit reading, but I was determined to read it through to see if comes alright in the end, the way I remembered it. Sadly, not.<br /><br />I have no desire to keep reading part 2 or book 2 or however “Endless Summer” is being advertised. I’ve had more than enough of Lori. And I still generally remember the many ways that she ups her antics with subterfuge in the second book. Lesson still not learned? No thank you to that. I don’t want to figuratively hang out with a character, for however many more pages, who can’t learn a very major lesson the first time. <br /><br />Another reviewer mentioned that YA should be something that can be enjoyed across all ages. And I agree. There are many well written YA books with complex characters that I still love. Hunger games, Harry Potter, anyone? To me, YA means that this book can be enjoyed by readers starting at this age and upwards. If a book falls into a category where it can only be enjoyed by a very limited age range… well… then it’s not a good book. And that’s my opinion. <br /><br />In conclusion, although I do see younger teens enjoying the drama in this book, I wouldn’t actually recommend it to anyone. Aside from the dialogue, some mildly funny narrative here and there, good representation of brotherly rivalry and competition, and those almost-moments where I thought the book might actually delve deeper and explore character, there wasn’t much to hold this book up. <br /><br />I am so turned off by part 1 that I don’t want to reread part 2. This was more than enough of memory lane for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6250600740810504109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_276922719" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/276922719" />
    <a title="Felicity" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1578484-felicity"><img alt="Felicity" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294784796p2/1578484.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/276922719?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Felicity" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Felicity" href="/user/show/1578484-felicity">Felicity</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1578484-felicity?shelf=funny">funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1578484-felicity?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1578484-felicity?shelf=cute-boy">cute-boy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer276922719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7922668372805314548"><i>The Boys Next Door and Endless Summer are going to get the same review, simply because I read both in one book which was called "Endless Summer". It wasn't until I was halfway through when I realized there were two parts to the book, "The Boys Next Door" as part one AND "Endless Summer" for part two. So in my mind, it's all kind of the same deal.</i><br/><br/>So I really loved The Boys Next Door. I thought Lori was an exceptionally funny, bright, young girl. She sounded like a really cool girl that I would ha</span>
  <span id="freeText7922668372805314548" style="display:none"><i>The Boys Next Door and Endless Summer are going to get the same review, simply because I read both in one book which was called "Endless Summer". It wasn't until I was halfway through when I realized there were two parts to the book, "The Boys Next Door" as part one AND "Endless Summer" for part two. So in my mind, it's all kind of the same deal.</i><br /><br />So I really loved The Boys Next Door. I thought Lori was an exceptionally funny, bright, young girl. She sounded like a really cool girl that I would have been friends with in highschool, because like her, I didn't do the 'dress up thing' either. And like Lori, I mostly hung out with boys, and only a small handful of girls (one girl in particular I was super close with). So I could totally relate to Lori in her eager attempt to present herself as a "real girl". When you reach that age, which is different for all girls, you don't want to be seen as just the cool girl that hangs out with the dudes.. You want some legitimate flirtatious action! I completely understood her desire, and I really enjoyed reading about her plans of attack with different themes. First it was drop the tankini, and enter the bikini. Then there was short shorts and shoes with high heels. Then there was a plan with make up. All plans were hilarious to read, and I felt that the way it was written was exceptionally true and really relayed the mindset of a 15 year old female on a mission to go from tomboy to girlfriend material. It was well done.<br /><br />I think Lori is exceptionally lucky to have an older brother like Bill. Many of my friends had older brothers and none of them were as nice or as friendly sounding as Bill.<br /><br />And the boys next door... well, they just sounded like they were a dream come true, especially (obviously) Adam. <br /><br />The Boys Next Door is all from Lori's opinion, and at times I thought it might be fun to hear it from Adam's side, but that is neither really hear nor there. It was a fun book to read, and I enjoyed the super sweet love story that eventually happened between Lori and Adam. The characters were fun, most importantly they were real (like they actually seemed they could exist in for real - real life, which is something you don't always see). Can I just say that I would kill for a job like they had? Working with boats by water all day and then go off and wakeboarding for an hour or so? SWEET!<br /><br /><br />Where I started to loose the happy-this-book-is-so-cute-feeling is in Endless Summer. Yes, I totally get that Lori and Adam are far too young to stay out all night. And as a single father, her dad acted rather appropriately in terms of being exceptionally angry. I didn't necessarily agree with how far he took it though. Forbidding Lori to see Adam? That's a bit much... Grounded? Yeah for sure, ground her! And then give her a curfew so she knows never to disobey like that ever again. But the whole "Adam is forbidden - no talking, no touching, no nothing" was too far for me. It was like Lori's dad completely forgot that these boys are his neighbours, and Lori and Bill have grown up with them their whole lives, and he has known these boys for their entire lives, and he's probably known their parents for even longer than that! None of these boys have ever purposefully hurt Lori, or were mean to her, or have ever done anything remotely awful. It's this one fretful night, which was an accident, and then it's the end of the world. "No more Adam from next door, he's a bad influence", etc. I'm not a parent, nor did I have an over protective parent growing up, so perhaps I have a hard time relating... Yes, Dad's angry and he has a right to be, but forbidding is a bit much. Which, of course, throws everything else into motion!<br /><br />Adam and Lori can't be together so they find ways to see one another. They go into the woods for a conversation together... they get caught. Lori sneaks out of her bedroom... Obviously, she gets caught! But none of it would've happened if her father had been a wee tiny bit more lenient in his sentecning for Lori. <br /><br />So throughout most of Endless Summer, I felt badly for poor Adam. First he can't see his girlfriend, his childhood friend who he's been wanting since gosh knows how long. They're only boyfriend and girlfriend for a few days before the whole incident of spending the night in the truck, and then they can't see each other anymore. Then he has all this brotherly tension to deal with between himself and his older brothes, especially the middle brother, Sean, who really isn't nice to Adam at all. Then there are his parents who are breathing down his neck, mostly because Lori's dad is breathing down their neck. Then there's the threat of military school. And I just feel so sorry for this guy. It must've been soooo frustrating for him. All the kid needs is a break! Adam is a good kid. Yes, he has a reputation for acting impulsively, which has caused himself harm from time to time, and yes, he probably doesn't always think through his actions or his words 100% before he goes through with them... but no one is perfect. I feel that everyone sort of picks on him (Sean incredibly so) because he has ADHD and does not have the easiest time focusing. There's this one moment between Adam and his father that is sweet, and I appreciate it, but I felt most of the book it was Adam against the world. No one was in his corner - not even Lori!<br /><br />To make matters worse, Lori becomes hell-bent on finding a different guy - a guy who is much less safer and crazier than Adam - to temporarily date so that her father can see how wonderful Adam really is in comparison. This idea obviously drives Adam nuts. And it drove me nuts too! In fact, Lori became significantly less cool in this half of the novel than she was in the first all because of this hairbrained idea. I get that she is only just sixteen, and thus a smide immature and rash at times, but I couldn't understand her reasoning. I wouldn't have seen that reasoning at 16 years old! She sees it that if she dates someone worse than Adam, her dad is going to come around. NO! It's only going to prove to her father that she doesn't really care about Adam all that much. Second, she has no regard for how that may hurt or affect Adam's feelings. Absolutely none. Obviously the plan backfires. <br /><br />What really needed to happen was what finally did at happen at the end, and that is because Adam is the much smater, more mature of the two. And Lori is careless, and foolhardy.<br /><br />It would be interesting to see Adam and Lori together again after college. I wouldn't expect them to stay together from 16 year olds to college graduates, but it would be really interesting to compare how they were in Endless Summer, to a different story where they're older. Perhaps a reflection of why they broke up, their life without each other, and then a romantic reunion... or something sweet. As it was, I felt that Adam really cared for Lori... but I never really felt that Lori shared the same feelings for Adam. In Boys Next Door, she's constantly after Sean but then realizes all her memories have been about Adam. Then in Endless Summer, she acts so insensitively, that it's really hard to see if she's into Adam, or into having any kind of serious boyfriend at all... Adam or not... since she just sort of looses that "I'm girlfriend material - not a Tomboy" mentality.<br /><br />Overall, an incredibly engaging, enjoyable read. It's only Lori's actions in Endless Summer that brings this 4 stars. It drove me mental that much. Imagine what it must've been like for Adam?!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7922668372805314548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12715725011323282984">Okay this was cute. I can't help it, I'm a sucker for these easy-to-read, light, fluffy, "butterflies in your stomach" type of "will they or won't they?" cutesy stories. I read this super fast and couldn't put it down. The first part was better than the second part. I probably would've been fine if it had ended right there, but I was glad it continued just so I could keep reading, but I thought everything about the second section was kind of ridiculous and unbelievable.   <span id="freeText12715725011323282984" style="display:none">Okay this was cute. I can't help it, I'm a sucker for these easy-to-read, light, fluffy, "butterflies in your stomach" type of "will they or won't they?" cutesy stories. I read this super fast and couldn't put it down. The first part was better than the second part. I probably would've been fine if it had ended right there, but I was glad it continued just so I could keep reading, but I thought everything about the second section was kind of ridiculous and unbelievable. Lori's little schemes drove me nuts. I'm not sure why she thought she was helping matters. The parents were all so over-the-top mad and strict about everything. But in general it was a super cute story and it made me smile.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0eGzPZgEWHHyidOd6IBm2jLKWtPw/NnzYVpbJRayPOM3lY6GPfQ3XUTm6RdNEK149vZ/G6TFnfdji0GGPIASZw==')})
    }
  }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer216267466334741042">Jennifer Echols was born in Atlanta and grew up in a small town on a beautiful lake in Alabama—a setting that has inspired many of her books. She has written nine romantic novels for young adults, including the comedy MAJOR CRUSH, which won the National Readers’ Choice Award, and the drama GOING TOO FAR, which was a finalist in the RITA, the National Readers’ Choice Award, and the Book Buyer’s Bes</span>
  <span id="freeText216267466334741042" style="display:none">Jennifer Echols was born in Atlanta and grew up in a small town on a beautiful lake in Alabama—a setting that has inspired many of her books. She has written nine romantic novels for young adults, including the comedy MAJOR CRUSH, which won the National Readers’ Choice Award, and the drama GOING TOO FAR, which was a finalist in the RITA, the National Readers’ Choice Award, and the Book Buyer’s Best, and was nominated by the American Library Association as a Best Book for Young Adults. Simon &amp; Schuster will debut her adult romance novels in 2013, with many more teen novels scheduled for the next few years. She lives in Birmingham with her husband and her son. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="216267466334741042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
